<p>A <b>mosque</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>; from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar"><span style="font-size:large;">المسجد</span></span>‎‎ <i>Al-masjid</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Place_of_worship" title="Place of worship">place of worship</a> for followers of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_nomad%27s_mosque_in_the_eastern_desert_of_Jordan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2988" data-file-width="5312" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/A_nomad%27s_mosque_in_the_eastern_desert_of_Jordan.jpg/260px-A_nomad%27s_mosque_in_the_eastern_desert_of_Jordan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/A_nomad%27s_mosque_in_the_eastern_desert_of_Jordan.jpg/390px-A_nomad%27s_mosque_in_the_eastern_desert_of_Jordan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/A_nomad%27s_mosque_in_the_eastern_desert_of_Jordan.jpg/520px-A_nomad%27s_mosque_in_the_eastern_desert_of_Jordan.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_nomad%27s_mosque_in_the_eastern_desert_of_Jordan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A nomad's mosque orientated towards Mecca (2016)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are strict and detailed requirements in <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> jurisprudence (<i><a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">fiqh</a></i>) for a place of worship to be considered a mosque, with places that do not meet these requirements regarded as <i>musalla</i>s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qaSunniPathFiqhMasjid_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-qaSunniPathFiqhMasjid-1">[1]</a></sup> There are stringent restrictions on the uses of the area formally demarcated as the mosque (which is often a small portion of the larger complex), and, in the Islamic <i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a></i> law, after an area is formally designated as a mosque, it remains so until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_the_Last_Judgment" title="Islamic view of the Last Judgment">Last Day</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qaSunniPathFiqhMasjid_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-qaSunniPathFiqhMasjid-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many mosques have elaborate <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">domes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minarets</a>, and prayer halls, in varying styles of architecture. Mosques originated on the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, but are now found in all inhabited continents. The mosque serves as a place where <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> can come together for <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">salat</a></i> (<span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar"><span style="font-size: 120%;">صلاة</span></span> <i>ṣalāt</i>, meaning "prayer") as well as a center for information, education, social welfare, and dispute settlement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">imam</a> leads the congregation in prayer.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word entered <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> from a <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> word that probably derived from <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> <i>moschea</i>, a variant of Italian <i>moscheta</i>, from either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Armenian_language" title="Middle Armenian language">Middle Armenian</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D5%B4%D5%A6%D5%AF%D5%AB%D5%A9" title="wikt:մզկիթ">մզկիթ</a> (<i>mzkit‘</i>) or <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Medieval Greek</a> μασγίδιον (<i>masgídion</i>) or Spanish <i>mezquita</i>, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar"><span style="font-size:large;">مسجد</span></span> <i>masjid</i> meaning "place of worship" or "prostration in prayer", either from <a href="/wiki/Nabataean_language" title="Nabataean language">Nabataean</a> <i>masg<sup>ĕ</sup>dhā́</i> or from Arabic <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar"><span style="font-size:large;">سجد</span></span> <i>sajada</i> meaning "to bow down in prayer", probably ultimately from <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a> <i>s<sup>ĕ</sup>ghēdh</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first mosque in the world is often considered to be the area around the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Haram" title="Masjid al-Haram">Masjid al-Haram</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Since as early as 638 AD, the Masjid al-Haram has been expanded on several occasions to accommodate the increasing number of Muslims who either live in the area or make the annual pilgrimage known as <i><a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">hajj</a></i> to the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Others regard the first mosque in history to be the <a href="/wiki/Quba_Mosque" title="Quba Mosque">Quba Mosque</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> since it was the first structure built by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> upon his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">emigration from Mecca</a> in 622.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2270" data-file-width="3280" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/220px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/330px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/440px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a>, in Tunisia, is the oldest mosque in the Muslim West.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Islamic Prophet Muhammad went on to establish another mosque in Medina, which is now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masjid_an-Nabawi" title="Masjid an-Nabawi">Masjid an-Nabawi</a>, or the Prophet's Mosque. Built on the site of his home, Muhammad participated in the construction of the mosque himself and helped pioneer the concept of the mosque as the focal point of the Islamic city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiu678_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-chiu678-7">[7]</a></sup> The Masjid al-Nabawi introduced some of the features still common in today's mosques, including the niche at the front of the prayer space known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Mihrab" title="Mihrab">mihrab</a></i> and the tiered pulpit called the <i><a href="/wiki/Minbar" title="Minbar">minbar</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The Masjid al-Nabawi was also constructed with a large courtyard, a motif common among mosques built since then.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chiu678_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-chiu678-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diffusion_and_evolution">Diffusion and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Diffusion and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sheikh_Lotfallah_Esfahan.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Sheikh_Lotfallah_Esfahan.JPG/220px-Sheikh_Lotfallah_Esfahan.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Sheikh_Lotfallah_Esfahan.JPG/330px-Sheikh_Lotfallah_Esfahan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Sheikh_Lotfallah_Esfahan.JPG/440px-Sheikh_Lotfallah_Esfahan.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sheikh_Lotfallah_Esfahan.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Lotfollah_Mosque" title="Sheikh Lotfollah Mosque">Sheikh Lotfollah Mosque</a> standing on the eastern side of Naghsh-i Jahan Square, Isfahan, Iran</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mosques had been built in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> by the end of the 7th century, as Islam spread outside the Arabian Peninsula with early <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">caliphates</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Imam_Husayn_Shrine" title="Imam Husayn Shrine">Imam Husayn Shrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karbala" title="Karbala">Karbala</a> is reportedly one of the oldest mosques in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, although its present form – typical of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_architecture" title="Persian architecture">Persian architecture</a> – only goes back to the 11th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The shrine, while still operating as a mosque, remains one of the holiest sites for <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Muslims</a>, as it honors the death of the third <a href="/wiki/Imamah_(Shia_doctrine)" title="Imamah (Shia doctrine)">Shia imam</a>, and Prophet Muhammad's grandson, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussein_ibn_Ali" title="Hussein ibn Ali">Hussein ibn Ali</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Amr_ibn_al-As" title="Mosque of Amr ibn al-As">Mosque of Amr ibn al-As</a> was reportedly the first mosque in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, serving as a religious and social center for <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>) during its prime. Like the Imam Husayn Shrine, though, nothing of its original structure remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> With the later Shia <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a>, mosques throughout Egypt evolved to include schools (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrasas</a></i>), hospitals, and tombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1_great_mosque_xian_2011.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/1_great_mosque_xian_2011.JPG/220px-1_great_mosque_xian_2011.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/1_great_mosque_xian_2011.JPG/330px-1_great_mosque_xian_2011.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/1_great_mosque_xian_2011.JPG/440px-1_great_mosque_xian_2011.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1_great_mosque_xian_2011.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Xi%27an" title="Great Mosque of Xi'an">Great Mosque of Xi'an</a> incorporates traditional elements of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Chinese architecture</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> was reportedly the first mosque built in northwest Africa, with its present form (dating from the 9th century) serving as a model for other Islamic places of worship in the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>. It was the first to incorporate a square <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minaret</a> (as opposed to the more common circular minaret) and includes <a href="/wiki/Nave" title="Nave">naves</a> akin to a <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elleh114_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-elleh114-13">[13]</a></sup> Those features can also be found in <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusian</a> mosques, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grand_Mosque_of_Cordoba" title="Grand Mosque of Cordoba">Grand Mosque of Cordoba</a>, as they tended to reflect the architecture of the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> instead of their <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">Visigoth</a> predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elleh114_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-elleh114-13">[13]</a></sup> Still, some elements of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visigothic_architecture" title="Visigothic architecture">Visigothic architecture</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_arch" title="Horseshoe arch">horseshoe arches</a>, were infused into the mosque architecture of Spain and the Maghreb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first mosque in East Asia was reportedly established in the 8th century in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Xi%27an" title="Great Mosque of Xi'an">Great Mosque of Xi'an</a>, whose current building dates from the 18th century, does not replicate the features often associated with mosques elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cow30_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-cow30-15">[15]</a></sup> Indeed, minarets were initially prohibited by the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahm109_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-ahm109-16">[16]</a></sup> Following traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Chinese architecture</a>, the Great Mosque of Xi'an, like many other mosques in eastern China, resembles a <a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">pagoda</a>, with a green roof instead of the yellow roof common on imperial structures in China. Mosques in western China were more likely to incorporate elements, like domes and minarets, traditionally seen in mosques elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cow30_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-cow30-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Imam_reza1_1_1_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="646" data-file-width="577" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Imam_reza1_1_1_1.jpg/220px-Imam_reza1_1_1_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Imam_reza1_1_1_1.jpg/330px-Imam_reza1_1_1_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Imam_reza1_1_1_1.jpg/440px-Imam_reza1_1_1_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Imam_reza1_1_1_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inside the <a href="/wiki/Imam_Reza_shrine" title="Imam Reza shrine">Imam Reza shrine</a> in Mashhad, Iran</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masjid-Kampung-Hulu-2364.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Masjid-Kampung-Hulu-2364.jpg/220px-Masjid-Kampung-Hulu-2364.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Masjid-Kampung-Hulu-2364.jpg/330px-Masjid-Kampung-Hulu-2364.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Masjid-Kampung-Hulu-2364.jpg/440px-Masjid-Kampung-Hulu-2364.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masjid-Kampung-Hulu-2364.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kampung_Hulu_Mosque" title="Kampung Hulu Mosque">Kampung Hulu Mosque</a>, the oldest mosque in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> influenced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malay_architecture" title="Malay architecture">Malay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">Chinese</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu_architecture" title="Hindu architecture">Hindu architecture</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A similar integration of foreign and local influences could be seen on the <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> islands of <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, where mosques, including the <a href="/wiki/Demak_Great_Mosque" title="Demak Great Mosque">Demak Great Mosque</a>, were first established in the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blobla439_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-blobla439-17">[17]</a></sup> Early Javanese mosques took design cues from <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple_architecture" title="Hindu temple architecture">Hindu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_architecture" title="Buddhist architecture">Buddhist</a>, and Chinese architectural influences, with tall timber, multi-level roofs similar to the pagodas of <a href="/wiki/Balinese_temple" title="Balinese temple">Balinese Hindu temples</a>; the ubiquitous Islamic dome did not appear in Indonesia until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahm109_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-ahm109-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In turn, the Javanese style influenced the styles of mosques in Indonesia's <a href="/wiki/Austronesia" title="Austronesia">Austronesian</a> neighbors—<a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blobla439_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-blobla439-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jama_Masjid,_Delhi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2445" data-file-width="3448" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Jama_Masjid%2C_Delhi.jpg/220px-Jama_Masjid%2C_Delhi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Jama_Masjid%2C_Delhi.jpg/330px-Jama_Masjid%2C_Delhi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Jama_Masjid%2C_Delhi.jpg/440px-Jama_Masjid%2C_Delhi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jama_Masjid,_Delhi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jama_Masjid,_Delhi" title="Jama Masjid, Delhi">Jama Masjid</a> in Delhi remains India's largest mosque and a classic example of the Mughal style of architecture.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Badshahi_Mosque_,_Lahore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2442" data-file-width="3104" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Badshahi_Mosque_%2C_Lahore.jpg/220px-Badshahi_Mosque_%2C_Lahore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Badshahi_Mosque_%2C_Lahore.jpg/330px-Badshahi_Mosque_%2C_Lahore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Badshahi_Mosque_%2C_Lahore.jpg/440px-Badshahi_Mosque_%2C_Lahore.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Badshahi_Mosque_,_Lahore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Badshahi_Mosque" title="Badshahi Mosque">Badshahi Mosque</a> (1673) in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, currently fifth largest mosque in the world</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Muslim empires were instrumental in the evolution and spread of mosques. Although mosques were first established in India during the 7th century, they were not commonplace across <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">the subcontinent</a> until the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughals</a> in the 16th and 17th centuries. Reflecting their <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid</a> origins, <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal-style</a> mosques included <a href="/wiki/Onion_dome" title="Onion dome">onion domes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ogee" title="Ogee">pointed arches</a>, and elaborate circular minarets, features common in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_architecture" title="Persian architecture">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Central_Asia" title="Architecture of Central Asia">Central Asian styles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jama_Masjid,_Delhi" title="Jama Masjid, Delhi">Jama Masjid</a> in <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Badshahi_Mosque" title="Badshahi Mosque">Badshahi Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, built in a similar manner in the mid-17th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> remain two of the largest mosques on the Indian subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> was particularly instrumental in spreading Islam and establishing mosques within the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, as the Umayyads constructed among the most revered mosques in the region—the <a href="/wiki/Al-Aqsa_Mosque" title="Al-Aqsa Mosque">al-Aqsa Mosque</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dome_of_the_Rock" title="Dome of the Rock">Dome of the Rock</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Mosque" title="Umayyad Mosque">Umayyad Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The designs of the Dome of the Rock and the Umayyad Mosque were influenced by <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a>, a trend that continued with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several of the early mosques in the Ottoman Empire were originally churches or cathedrals from the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> (one of those converted cathedrals) informing the architecture of mosques from after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">Ottoman conquest of Constantinople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Still, the Ottomans developed <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_architecture" title="Ottoman architecture">their own architectural style</a> characterized by large central rotundas (sometimes surrounded by multiple smaller domes), pencil-shaped minarets, and open <a href="/wiki/Facade" title="Facade">facades</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mosques from the Ottoman period are still scattered across Eastern Europe, but the most rapid growth in the number of mosques in Europe has occurred within the past century as more Muslims have migrated to the continent. Many major European cities are home to mosques, like the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Mosque_of_Paris" title="Grand Mosque of Paris">Grand Mosque of Paris</a>, that incorporate domes, minarets, and other features often found with mosques in Muslim-majority countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blobla193_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-blobla193-26">[26]</a></sup> The first mosque in North America was founded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albanian_American" title="Albanian American">Albanian Americans</a> in 1915, but the continent's oldest surviving mosque, the <a href="/wiki/Mother_Mosque_of_America" title="Mother Mosque of America">Mother Mosque of America</a>, only dates back to the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> As in Europe, the number of American mosques has rapidly increased in recent decades as Muslim immigrants, particularly from South Asia, have come in the United States. Greater than forty percent of mosques in the United States were constructed after 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_of_places_of_worship">Conversion of places of worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Conversion of places of worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conversion_of_non-Muslim_places_of_worship_into_mosques" title="Conversion of non-Muslim places of worship into mosques">Conversion of non-Muslim places of worship into mosques</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turkey-3019_-_Hagia_Sophia_(2216460729).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2534" data-file-width="3329" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Turkey-3019_-_Hagia_Sophia_%282216460729%29.jpg/220px-Turkey-3019_-_Hagia_Sophia_%282216460729%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Turkey-3019_-_Hagia_Sophia_%282216460729%29.jpg/330px-Turkey-3019_-_Hagia_Sophia_%282216460729%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Turkey-3019_-_Hagia_Sophia_%282216460729%29.jpg/440px-Turkey-3019_-_Hagia_Sophia_%282216460729%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turkey-3019_-_Hagia_Sophia_(2216460729).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a> was converted into a mosque after the Ottoman conquest of Constantinople in 1453</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to early <a href="/wiki/List_of_Muslim_historians" title="List of Muslim historians">Muslim historians</a>, towns that surrendered without resistance and made treaties with the Muslims gave the Muslims permission to take their churches and <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogues</a>. One of the earliest examples of these kinds of conversions was in <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, Syria, where in 705 <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Walid_I" title="Al-Walid I">Al-Walid I</a> bought the church of <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">St. John</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a> and had it rebuilt as a mosque in exchange for building a number of new churches for the Christians in Damascus. Overall, <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Malik_ibn_Marwan" title="Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan">Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan</a> (Al-Waleed's father) is said to have transformed 10 churches in Damascus into mosques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The process of turning churches into mosques was especially intensive in the villages where most of the inhabitants <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">converted</a> to Islam. The <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">al-Ma'mun</a> turned many churches into mosques. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman Turks</a> converted nearly all churches, monasteries, and chapels in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, including the famous <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, into mosques immediately after <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">capturing the city in 1453</a>. In some instances mosques have been established on the places of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a> or Christian sanctuaries associated with <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Biblical</a> personalities who were also recognized by Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masdjid1_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Masdjid1-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mosques have also been converted for use by other religions, notably in southern Spain, following the conquest of the Moors in 1492.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The most prominent of them is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral%E2%80%93Mosque_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Cathedral–Mosque of Córdoba">Great Mosque of Cordoba</a>. Outside of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, such instances also occurred in southeastern Europe once regions were no longer under Muslim rule.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_functions">Religious functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Religious functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <i>masjid jāmi'</i>, a central mosque, can play a role in religious activities such as teaching the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and educating future imams.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prayers">Prayers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Prayers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Istiqlal_Mosque_Eid_ul_Fitr_Jamaah_2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="1701" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Istiqlal_Mosque_Eid_ul_Fitr_Jamaah_2.JPG/220px-Istiqlal_Mosque_Eid_ul_Fitr_Jamaah_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Istiqlal_Mosque_Eid_ul_Fitr_Jamaah_2.JPG/330px-Istiqlal_Mosque_Eid_ul_Fitr_Jamaah_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Istiqlal_Mosque_Eid_ul_Fitr_Jamaah_2.JPG/440px-Istiqlal_Mosque_Eid_ul_Fitr_Jamaah_2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Istiqlal_Mosque_Eid_ul_Fitr_Jamaah_2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inside the <a href="/wiki/Istiqlal_Mosque,_Jakarta" title="Istiqlal Mosque, Jakarta">Istiqlal Mosque, Jakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eid_ul-Fitr" title="Eid ul-Fitr">Eid ul-Fitr</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are two holidays (<i>Eids</i>) in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic calendar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eid_ul-Fitr" title="Eid ul-Fitr">Eid ul-Fitr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Eid al-Adha</a>, during which there are special prayers held at mosques in the morning. These <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat_al_Eid" title="Salat al Eid">Eid prayers</a> are supposed to be offered in large groups, and so, in the absence of an outdoor <i><a href="/wiki/Eidgah" title="Eidgah">eidgah</a></i> larger mosques will normally host them for their congregants as well as the congregants of smaller local mosques. Some mosques will even rent <a href="/wiki/Convention_center" title="Convention center">convention centers</a> or other large public buildings to hold the large number of Muslims who attend. Mosques, especially those in countries where Muslims are the majority, will also host Eid prayers outside in courtyards, <a href="/wiki/Town_square" title="Town square">town squares</a> or on the outskirts of town in an <i><a href="/wiki/Eidgah" title="Eidgah">Eidgah</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ramadan_events">Ramadan events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ramadan events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Islam's holiest month, <i><a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a></i>, is observed through many events. As Muslims must <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sawm" title="Sawm">fast</a> during the day during Ramadan, mosques will host <i><a href="/wiki/Iftar" title="Iftar">iftar</a></i> dinners after sunset and the fourth required prayer of the day, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maghrib" title="Maghrib">maghrib</a></i>. Food is provided, at least in part, by members of the community, thereby creating daily <a href="/wiki/Potluck" title="Potluck">potluck</a> dinners. Because of the community contribution necessary to serve iftar dinners, mosques with smaller congregations may not be able to host the <i>iftar</i> dinners daily. Some mosques will also hold <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suhoor" title="Suhoor">suhoor</a></i> meals before <a href="/wiki/Dawn" title="Dawn">dawn</a> to congregants attending the first required prayer of the day, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fajr" title="Fajr">fajr</a></i>. As with iftar dinners, congregants usually provide the food for suhoor, although able mosques may provide food instead. Mosques will often invite poorer members of the Muslim community to share in beginning and breaking the fasts, as providing <a href="/wiki/Alms" title="Alms">charity</a> during Ramadan is regarded in Islam as especially honorable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Fast_Break.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1208" data-file-width="1610" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Fast_Break.JPG/220px-Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Fast_Break.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Fast_Break.JPG/330px-Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Fast_Break.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Fast_Break.JPG/440px-Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Fast_Break.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Fast_Break.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Iftar" title="Iftar">Iftar</a> at <a href="/wiki/Taipei_Grand_Mosque" title="Taipei Grand Mosque">Taipei Grand Mosque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> during <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the last obligatory daily prayer (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isha%27" title="Isha'">Isha'</a></i>) special, optional <i><a href="/wiki/Tarawih" title="Tarawih">tarawih</a></i> prayers are offered in larger mosques. During each night of prayers, which can last for up to two hours each night, usually one member of the community who has memorized the entire Quran (a <a href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Quran)" title="Hafiz (Quran)">Hafiz</a>) will recite a segment of the book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teach-islam_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-teach-islam-35">[35]</a></sup> Sometimes, several such people (not necessarily of the local community) take turns to do this. During the last ten days of Ramadan, larger mosques will host all-night programs to observe <a href="/wiki/Laylat_al-Qadr" title="Laylat al-Qadr">Laylat al-Qadr</a>, the night Muslims believe that Muhammad first received Quranic revelations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teach-islam_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-teach-islam-35">[35]</a></sup> On that night, between <a href="/wiki/Sunset" title="Sunset">sunset</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunrise" title="Sunrise">sunrise</a>, mosques employ speakers to educate congregants in attendance about Islam. Mosques or the community usually provide meals periodically throughout the night</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nasr_ol_Molk_mosque_vault_ceiling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Nasr_ol_Molk_mosque_vault_ceiling.jpg/220px-Nasr_ol_Molk_mosque_vault_ceiling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Nasr_ol_Molk_mosque_vault_ceiling.jpg/330px-Nasr_ol_Molk_mosque_vault_ceiling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Nasr_ol_Molk_mosque_vault_ceiling.jpg/440px-Nasr_ol_Molk_mosque_vault_ceiling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nasr_ol_Molk_mosque_vault_ceiling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Mulk_Mosque" title="Nasir al-Mulk Mosque">Nasir al-Mulk Mosque</a> vault ceiling Shiraz,Iran</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the last ten days of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan_(calendar_month)" title="Ramadan (calendar month)">Ramadan</a>, larger mosques within the Muslim community will host <i><a href="/wiki/I%CA%BFtik%C4%81f" title="Iʿtikāf">Iʿtikāf</a></i>, a practice in which at least one Muslim man from the community must participate. Muslims performing itikaf are required to stay within the mosque for ten consecutive days, often in worship or learning about Islam. As a result, the rest of the Muslim community is responsible for providing the participants with food, drinks, and whatever else they need during their stay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teach-islam_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-teach-islam-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity">Charity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Charity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The third of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Pillars_of_Islam" title="Five Pillars of Islam">Five Pillars of Islam</a> states that Muslims are required to give approximately one-fortieth of their wealth to charity as <i><a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">zakat</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Since mosques form the center of Muslim communities, they are where Muslims go to both give <i>zakat</i> and, if necessary, collect it. Before the holiday of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eid_ul-Fitr" title="Eid ul-Fitr">Eid ul-Fitr</a>, mosques also collect a special <i>zakat</i> that is supposed to assist in helping poor Muslims attend the prayers and celebrations associated with the holiday.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_political_roles">Contemporary political roles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Contemporary political roles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Political_aspects_of_Islam" title="Political aspects of Islam">Political aspects of Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_East_London_Mosque_complex_-_Feb_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="953" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Aerial_view_of_East_London_Mosque_complex_-_Feb_2014.jpg/220px-Aerial_view_of_East_London_Mosque_complex_-_Feb_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Aerial_view_of_East_London_Mosque_complex_-_Feb_2014.jpg/330px-Aerial_view_of_East_London_Mosque_complex_-_Feb_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Aerial_view_of_East_London_Mosque_complex_-_Feb_2014.jpg/440px-Aerial_view_of_East_London_Mosque_complex_-_Feb_2014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_East_London_Mosque_complex_-_Feb_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/East_London_Mosque" title="East London Mosque">East London Mosque</a> was one of the first in Britain to be allowed to use <a href="/wiki/Loudspeakers_in_mosques" title="Loudspeakers in mosques">loudspeakers</a> to broadcast the adhan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The late 20th century saw an increase in the number of mosques used for political purposes. Today, civic participation is commonly promoted in mosques in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>. Because of the importance in the community, mosques are used for preaching peaceful coexistence with non-believers, even in times of adversity.</p>
<p>Large mosques sometimes play a political role as well. In Islamic countries like Bangladesh, Pakistan, Iran, and Saudi Arabia, political subjects are preached by imams at Friday congregations on a regular basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In other Islamic countries, imams are usually banned from mentioning political issues.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advocacy">Advocacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Advocacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Countries with a minority Muslim population are more likely than Muslim-majority countries of the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Middle_East" title="Greater Middle East">Greater Middle East</a> to use mosques as a way to promote civic participation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tc_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-tc-39">[39]</a></sup> American mosques host <a href="/wiki/Voter_registration" title="Voter registration">voter registration</a> and civic participation drives that promote involving Muslims, who are often first- or second-generation immigrants, in the political process. As a result of these efforts as well as attempts at mosques to keep Muslims informed about the issues facing the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a>, regular mosque attendants are more likely to participate in <a href="/wiki/Protest" title="Protest">protests</a>, sign <a href="/wiki/Petition" title="Petition">petitions</a>, and otherwise be involved in politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tc_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-tc-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nevertheless, a link between <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">political views</a> and mosque attendance can still be seen in other parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2006_al-Askari_Mosque_bombing" title="2006 al-Askari Mosque bombing">al-Askari Mosque bombing</a> in February 2006, imams and other Islamic leaders used mosques and <a href="/wiki/Jumu%27ah" title="Jumu'ah">Friday prayers</a> as vehicles to call for calm and peace in the midst of widespread violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_conflict">Social conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Social conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamophobia" title="Islamophobia">Islamophobia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosque_in_Sarajevo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2126" data-file-width="1936" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Mosque_in_Sarajevo.jpg/220px-Mosque_in_Sarajevo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Mosque_in_Sarajevo.jpg/330px-Mosque_in_Sarajevo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Mosque_in_Sarajevo.jpg/440px-Mosque_in_Sarajevo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mosque_in_Sarajevo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mosque in <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:English_Defence_League_protest_in_Newcastle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/English_Defence_League_protest_in_Newcastle.jpg/220px-English_Defence_League_protest_in_Newcastle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/English_Defence_League_protest_in_Newcastle.jpg/330px-English_Defence_League_protest_in_Newcastle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/English_Defence_League_protest_in_Newcastle.jpg/440px-English_Defence_League_protest_in_Newcastle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:English_Defence_League_protest_in_Newcastle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Protest by the <a href="/wiki/English_Defence_League" title="English Defence League">English Defence League</a> calling for a mosque in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newcastle-upon-Tyne" title="Newcastle-upon-Tyne">Newcastle-upon-Tyne</a> to be shut down</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As they are considered important to the Muslim community, mosques, like other places of worship, can be at the heart of social conflicts. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babri_Mosque" title="Babri Mosque">Babri Mosque</a> was the subject of such a conflict up until the early 1990s when it was demolished. Before a mutual solution could be devised, the mosque was destroyed on December 6, 1992 as the mosque was built by <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> allegedly on the site of a previous <a href="/wiki/Hindu_temple" title="Hindu temple">Hindu temple</a> marking the birthplace of <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The controversy surrounded the mosque was directly linked to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bombay_Riots" title="Bombay Riots">rioting in Bombay</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>) as well as <a href="/wiki/1993_Bombay_bombings" title="1993 Bombay bombings">bombings in 1993</a> that killed 257 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bombings in February 2006 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007_al-Askari_Mosque_bombing" title="2007 al-Askari Mosque bombing">June 2007</a> seriously damaged Iraq's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Askari_Mosque" title="Al-Askari Mosque">al-Askari Mosque</a> and exacerbated existing tensions. Other mosque bombings in Iraq, both before and after the February 2006 bombing, have been part of the conflict between the country's groups of Muslims. However, mosque bombings have not been exclusive to Iraq; in June 2005, a <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attack" title="Suicide attack">suicide bomber</a> killed at least 19 people at an Afghan <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia</a> mosque near Jade Maivand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In April 2006, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2006_Jama_Masjid_explosions" title="2006 Jama Masjid explosions">two explosions</a> occurred at India's Jama Masjid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>, several American mosques were targeted in attacks ranging from simple <a href="/wiki/Vandalism" title="Vandalism">vandalism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Arson" title="Arson">arson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Furthermore, the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Defense_League" title="Jewish Defense League">Jewish Defense League</a> was suspected of plotting to bomb the King Fahd Mosque in <a href="/wiki/Culver_City,_California" title="Culver City, California">Culver City, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Similar attacks occurred throughout the United Kingdom following the <a href="/wiki/7_July_2005_London_bombings" title="7 July 2005 London bombings">7 July 2005 London bombings</a>. Outside the Western world, in June 2001, the <a href="/wiki/Hassan_Bek_Mosque" title="Hassan Bek Mosque">Hassan Bek Mosque</a> was the target of vandalism and attacks by hundreds of Israelis after a suicide bomber killed 19 people in a night club in Tel Aviv.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Although mosquegoing is highly encouraged for men, it is permitted to stay at home when one feels at risk from Islamophobic persecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saudi_influence">Saudi influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Saudi influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Faisal_mosque2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="795" data-file-width="1136" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faisal_mosque2.jpg/220px-Faisal_mosque2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faisal_mosque2.jpg/330px-Faisal_mosque2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Faisal_mosque2.jpg/440px-Faisal_mosque2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Faisal_mosque2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Funded by King <a href="/wiki/Faisal_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Faisal of Saudi Arabia">Faisal of Saudi Arabia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Faisal_Mosque" title="Faisal Mosque">Faisal Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Islamabad" title="Islamabad">Islamabad</a> is the largest mosque in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although the Saudi involvement in Sunni mosques around the world can be traced back to the 1960s, it was not until later in the 20th century that the government of Saudi Arabia became a large influence in foreign Sunni mosques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-money-trails_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-money-trails-53">[53]</a></sup> Beginning in the 1980s, the Saudi Arabian government began to finance the construction of Sunni mosques in countries around the world. An estimated <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>45 billion has been spent by the Saudi Arabian government financing mosques and Sunni Islamic schools in foreign countries. <i><a href="/wiki/Ain_al-Yaqeen" title="Ain al-Yaqeen">Ain al-Yaqeen</a></i>, a Saudi newspaper, reported in 2002 that Saudi funds may have contributed to building as many as 1,500 mosques and 2,000 other Islamic centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>Saudi citizens have also contributed significantly to mosques in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a>, especially in countries where they see Muslims as poor and oppressed. Following the fall of the Soviet Union, in 1992, mosques in war-torn Afghanistan saw many contributions from Saudi citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-money-trails_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-money-trails-53">[53]</a></sup> The King Fahd Mosque in Culver City, California and the Islamic Cultural Center of Italy in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> represent two of Saudi Arabia's largest investments in foreign mosques as former Saudi king <a href="/wiki/Fahd_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Fahd of Saudi Arabia">Fahd bin Abdul Aziz al-Saud</a> contributed US$8 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-money-trails_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-money-trails-53">[53]</a></sup> and US$50 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> to the two mosques, respectively.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Styles">Styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2270" data-file-width="3280" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/220px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/330px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/440px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> is one of the earliest remaining mosques.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Arab-plan</i> or <a href="/wiki/Hypostyle" title="Hypostyle">hypostyle</a> mosques are the earliest type of mosques, pioneered under the Umayyad Dynasty. These mosques have square or rectangular plans with an enclosed courtyard and covered prayer hall. Historically, in the warm Middle Eastern and <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climates</a>, the courtyard served to accommodate the large number of worshippers during Friday prayers. Most early hypostyle mosques had flat roofs on prayer halls, which required the use of numerous <a href="/wiki/Column" title="Column">columns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Support_(structure)" title="Support (structure)">supports</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masdjid1_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Masdjid1-30">[30]</a></sup> One of the most notable hypostyle mosques is the Great Mosque of Cordoba in Spain, the building being supported by over 850 columns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit-handout_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-mit-handout-56">[56]</a></sup> Frequently, hypostyle mosques have outer <a href="/wiki/Arcade_(architecture)" title="Arcade (architecture)">arcades</a> so that visitors can enjoy the shade. Arab-plan mosques were constructed mostly under the Umayyad and Abbasid dynasties; subsequently, however, the simplicity of the Arab plan limited the opportunities for further development, the mosques consequently losing popularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masdjid1_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Masdjid1-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isfahan_Royal_Mosque_general.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Isfahan_Royal_Mosque_general.JPG/220px-Isfahan_Royal_Mosque_general.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Isfahan_Royal_Mosque_general.JPG/330px-Isfahan_Royal_Mosque_general.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Isfahan_Royal_Mosque_general.JPG/440px-Isfahan_Royal_Mosque_general.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isfahan_Royal_Mosque_general.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Shah_Mosque" title="Shah Mosque">Shah Mosque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first departure within mosque design started in Persia (Iran). The <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a> had inherited a rich <a href="/wiki/Iranian_architecture" title="Iranian architecture">architectural legacy</a> from the earlier Persian dynasties, and they began incorporating elements from earlier <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid</a> designs into their mosques, influenced by buildings such as the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Ardashir" title="Palace of Ardashir">Palace of Ardashir</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sarvestan_Palace" title="Sarvestan Palace">Sarvestan Palace</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-57">[57]</a></sup> Thus, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_architecture" title="Islamic architecture">Islamic architecture</a> witnessed the introduction of such structures as domes and large, arched entrances, referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Iwan" title="Iwan">iwans</a></i>. During <a href="/wiki/Seljuq_dynasty" title="Seljuq dynasty">Seljuq rule</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Islamic mysticism</a> was on the rise, the <a href="/wiki/Shah_Mosque#Design_.E2.80.93_the_four-iwan_style" title="Shah Mosque">four-iwan</a> arrangement took form. The four-iwan format, finalized by the Seljuqs, and later inherited by the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavids</a>, firmly established the courtyard façade of such mosques, with the towering gateways at every side, as more important than the actual buildings themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-57">[57]</a></sup> They typically took the form of a square-shaped central courtyard with large entrances at each side, giving the impression of gateways to the spiritual world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The Persians also introduced <a href="/wiki/Persian_gardens" title="Persian gardens">Persian gardens</a> into mosque designs. Soon, a distinctly <a href="/wiki/Iranian_architecture" title="Iranian architecture">Persian style</a> of mosques started appearing that would significantly influence the designs of later <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid</a>, and also <a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal</a>, mosque designs.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C4%B0stanbul_-_Yavuz_Selim_Camii_-_Mart_2013_-_r2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/%C4%B0stanbul_-_Yavuz_Selim_Camii_-_Mart_2013_-_r2.JPG/220px-%C4%B0stanbul_-_Yavuz_Selim_Camii_-_Mart_2013_-_r2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/%C4%B0stanbul_-_Yavuz_Selim_Camii_-_Mart_2013_-_r2.JPG/330px-%C4%B0stanbul_-_Yavuz_Selim_Camii_-_Mart_2013_-_r2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/%C4%B0stanbul_-_Yavuz_Selim_Camii_-_Mart_2013_-_r2.JPG/440px-%C4%B0stanbul_-_Yavuz_Selim_Camii_-_Mart_2013_-_r2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C4%B0stanbul_-_Yavuz_Selim_Camii_-_Mart_2013_-_r2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Yavuz_Selim_Mosque" title="Yavuz Selim Mosque">Yavuz Selim Mosque</a>, an Ottoman mosque in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Ottomans introduced central dome mosques in the 15th century. These mosques have a large dome centered over the prayer hall. In addition to having a large central dome, a common feature is smaller domes that exist off-center over the prayer hall or throughout the rest of the mosque, where prayer is not performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit-vocab_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-mit-vocab-59">[59]</a></sup> This style was heavily influenced by Byzantine architecture with its use of large central domes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masdjid1_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Masdjid1-30">[30]</a></sup> Hajja Soad's mosque took a <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramid</a> shape that is a creative style in Islamic architecture.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Faisal_Mosque" title="Faisal Mosque">Faisal Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Islamabad" title="Islamabad">Islamabad</a>, Pakistan, in a relatively unusual design fuses contemporary lines with the more traditional look of an Arab <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a>'s tent, with its large triangular prayer hall and four minarets. However, unlike traditional mosque design, it lacks a dome. The mosque's architecture is a departure from the long history of South Asian Islamic architecture. It is one of the most outstanding and modern Islamic architecture examples in the world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2016)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Puffery" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="Unverified descriptions (December 2016)">peacock term</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Mosques built in Southeast Asia often represent the <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Indonesia" title="Architecture of Indonesia">Indonesian-Javanese style architecture</a>, which are different from the ones found throughout the Greater Middle East. The ones found in Europe and North America appear to have various styles but most are built on Western architectural designs, some are former churches or other buildings that were used by non-Muslims. In Africa, most mosques are old but the new ones are built in imitation of those of the Middle East. This can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Abuja_National_Mosque" title="Abuja National Mosque">Abuja National Mosque</a> in Nigeria and others.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minarets">Minarets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Minarets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tower_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tower_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan.JPG/150px-Tower_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tower_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan.JPG/225px-Tower_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Tower_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan.JPG/300px-Tower_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tower_of_the_Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The oldest standing minaret in the world at the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mevlid-i_Halil_Mosque_17.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4716" data-file-width="3095" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Mevlid-i_Halil_Mosque_17.jpg/150px-Mevlid-i_Halil_Mosque_17.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Mevlid-i_Halil_Mosque_17.jpg/225px-Mevlid-i_Halil_Mosque_17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Mevlid-i_Halil_Mosque_17.jpg/300px-Mevlid-i_Halil_Mosque_17.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mevlid-i_Halil_Mosque_17.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Typical Ottoman minarets in Turkey</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A common feature in mosques is the <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minaret</a>, the tall, slender tower that usually is situated at one of the corners of the mosque structure. The top of the minaret is always the highest point in mosques that have one, and often the highest point in the immediate area. The tallest minaret in the world is located at the <a href="/wiki/Hassan_II_Mosque" title="Hassan II Mosque">Hassan II Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Casablanca" title="Casablanca">Casablanca</a>, Morocco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reference_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reference-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hassan_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hassan-62">[62]</a></sup> It has a height of 210 metres (689 ft) and completed in 1993, it was designed by <a href="/wiki/Michel_Pinseau" title="Michel Pinseau">Michel Pinseau</a>. The first mosques had no minarets, and even nowadays the most conservative Islamic movements, like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahhabi" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabis</a>, avoid building minarets, seeing them as ostentatious and <a href="/wiki/Hazard" title="Hazard">hazardous</a> in case of collapse.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (February 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Mosque#Dubious" title="Talk:Mosque">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>The first minaret was constructed in 665 in <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> during the reign of the Umayyad caliph <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah I</a>. Muawiyah encouraged the construction of minarets, as they were supposed to bring mosques on par with Christian churches with their <a href="/wiki/Bell_tower" title="Bell tower">bell towers</a>. Consequently, mosque architects borrowed the shape of the bell tower for their minarets, which were used for essentially the same purpose—calling the faithful to prayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manara_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manara-63">[63]</a></sup> The oldest standing minaret in the world is the minaret of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Uqba" title="Mosque of Uqba">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> in Tunisia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> built between the 8th and the 9th century, it is a massive square tower consisting of three superimposed tiers of gradual size and decor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Before the five required daily prayers, a <i><a href="/wiki/Muezzin" title="Muezzin">muezzin</a></i> calls the worshippers to prayer from the minaret. In many countries like Singapore where Muslims are not the majority, mosques are prohibited from loudly broadcasting the call to prayer (<i><a href="/wiki/Adhan" title="Adhan">adhan</a></i>), although it is supposed to be said loudly to the surrounding community. The <i>adhan</i> is required before every prayer. However, nearly every mosque assigns a <i>muezzin</i> for each prayer to say the <i>adhan</i> as it is a recommended practice or <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">Islamic prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>. At mosques that do not have minarets, the <i>adhan</i> is called instead from inside the mosque or somewhere else on the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teach-islam_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-teach-islam-35">[35]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Iqama" title="Iqama">iqama</a></i>, which is similar to the <i>adhan</i> and said immediately before the start of prayer, is usually not said from the minaret even if a mosque has one.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mihrab">Mihrab</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mihrab">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1043" data-file-width="929" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg/200px-Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg/300px-Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg/400px-Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haga_Sofia_RB5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mihrab in <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Sophia" title="Hagia Sophia">Hagia Sophia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Mihrab" title="Mihrab">mihrab</a>, also spelled as <i>mehrab</i> is a semicircular <a href="/wiki/Niche_(architecture)" title="Niche (architecture)">niche</a> in the wall of a mosque that indicates the <i><a href="/wiki/Qibla" title="Qibla">qibla</a></i>. That is, the direction of the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and hence the direction that <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> should face when praying. The wall in which a <i>mihrab</i> appears is thus the "<i>qibla</i> wall."</p>
<p><i>Mihrab</i>s should not be confused with the <i><a href="/wiki/Minbar" title="Minbar">minbar</a></i>, which is the raised platform from which an <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a> (leader of prayer) addresses the congregation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domes">Domes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Domes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dome_Chhatris_Spires_-_Taj_Mahal_-_Agra_2014-05-14_3805.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3849" data-file-width="5790" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dome_Chhatris_Spires_-_Taj_Mahal_-_Agra_2014-05-14_3805.JPG/220px-Dome_Chhatris_Spires_-_Taj_Mahal_-_Agra_2014-05-14_3805.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dome_Chhatris_Spires_-_Taj_Mahal_-_Agra_2014-05-14_3805.JPG/330px-Dome_Chhatris_Spires_-_Taj_Mahal_-_Agra_2014-05-14_3805.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Dome_Chhatris_Spires_-_Taj_Mahal_-_Agra_2014-05-14_3805.JPG/440px-Dome_Chhatris_Spires_-_Taj_Mahal_-_Agra_2014-05-14_3805.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dome_Chhatris_Spires_-_Taj_Mahal_-_Agra_2014-05-14_3805.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mughal_architecture" title="Mughal architecture">Mughal</a>-style onion-shaped dome of <a href="/wiki/Taj_Mahal" title="Taj Mahal">Taj Mahal</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The white Taj Mahal building is not a mosque but a tomb. How does this image suit the article? (December 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The domes, often placed directly above the main prayer hall, may signify the vaults of <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">heaven</a> and the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> As time progressed, domes grew, from occupying a small part of the roof near the mihrab to encompassing the whole roof above the prayer hall. Although domes normally took on the shape of a hemisphere, the Mughals in India popularized onion-shaped domes in South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Some mosques have multiple, often smaller, domes in addition to the main large dome that resides at the center.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prayer_hall">Prayer hall</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Prayer hall">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The prayer hall, also known as the <i>musallah</i>, rarely has furniture; chairs and pews are generally absent from the prayer hall so as to allow as many worshipers as possible to line the room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unitulsa_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-unitulsa-69">[69]</a></sup> Some mosques have <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic calligraphy</a> and Quranic verses on the walls to assist worshippers in focusing on the beauty of Islam and its holiest book, the Quran, as well as for decoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teach-islam_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-teach-islam-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
