<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan,_prayer_hall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2996" data-file-width="4385" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan%2C_prayer_hall.jpg/220px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan%2C_prayer_hall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan%2C_prayer_hall.jpg/330px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan%2C_prayer_hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan%2C_prayer_hall.jpg/440px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan%2C_prayer_hall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan,_prayer_hall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The hypostyle prayer hall in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Often, a limited part of the prayer hall is sanctified formally as a masjid in the sharia sense (although the term masjid is also used for the larger mosque complex as well). Once designated, there are onerous limitations on the use of this formally designated masjid, and it may not be used for any purpose other than worship; restrictions that do not necessarily apply to the rest of the prayer area, and to the rest of the mosque complex (although such uses may be restricted by the conditions of the <i><a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">waqf</a></i> that owns the mosque).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qaSunniPathFiqhMasjid_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-qaSunniPathFiqhMasjid-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>In many mosques, especially the early congregational mosques, the prayer hall is in the hypostyle form (the roof held up by a multitude of columns).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> One of the finest examples of the hypostyle-plan mosques is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Uqba" title="Mosque of Uqba">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> (also known as the Mosque of Uqba) in Tunisia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Usually opposite the entrance to the prayer hall is the <i><a href="/wiki/Qibla" title="Qibla">qiblah</a></i> wall, the visually emphasized area inside the prayer hall. The qiblah wall should, in a properly oriented mosque, be set perpendicular to a line leading to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, the location of the <i><a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Congregants pray in rows parallel to the qiblah wall and thus arrange themselves so they face Mecca. In the qiblah wall, usually at its center, is the mihrab, a niche or depression indicating the direction of Mecca. Usually the mihrab is not occupied by furniture either. Sometimes, especially during Friday prayers, a raised <i><a href="/wiki/Minbar" title="Minbar">minbar</a></i> or pulpit is located to the side of the mihrab for a <i><a href="/wiki/Khatib" title="Khatib">khatib</a></i> or some other speaker to offer a sermon (<i><a href="/wiki/Khutbah" title="Khutbah">khutbah</a></i>). The mihrab serves as the location where the imam leads the five daily prayers on a regular basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ablution_facilities">Ablution facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ablution facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ablution_area_inside_Eastern_wall_of_Badshahi_mosque.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ablution_area_inside_Eastern_wall_of_Badshahi_mosque.JPG/220px-Ablution_area_inside_Eastern_wall_of_Badshahi_mosque.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ablution_area_inside_Eastern_wall_of_Badshahi_mosque.JPG/330px-Ablution_area_inside_Eastern_wall_of_Badshahi_mosque.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Ablution_area_inside_Eastern_wall_of_Badshahi_mosque.JPG/440px-Ablution_area_inside_Eastern_wall_of_Badshahi_mosque.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ablution_area_inside_Eastern_wall_of_Badshahi_mosque.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Wudu" title="Wudu">wudu</a></i> ("ablution") area, where Muslims wash their hands, forearm, face and feet before they pray. Example from the <a href="/wiki/Badshahi_Mosque" title="Badshahi Mosque">Badshahi Mosque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification#Islam" title="Ritual purification">ritual purification</a> precedes all prayers, mosques often have <a href="/wiki/Wudu" title="Wudu">ablution</a> fountains or other facilities for washing in their entryways or courtyards. However, worshippers at much smaller mosques often have to use restrooms to perform their ablutions. In traditional mosques, this function is often elaborated into a freestanding building in the center of a courtyard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit-handout_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-mit-handout-56">[56]</a></sup> This desire for cleanliness extends to the prayer halls where shoes are disallowed to be worn anywhere other than the cloakroom. Thus, foyers with shelves to put shoes and racks to hold coats are commonplace among mosques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unitulsa_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-unitulsa-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_features">Contemporary features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Contemporary features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Modern mosques have a variety of amenities available to their congregants. As mosques are supposed to appeal to the community, they may also have additional facilities, from <a href="/wiki/Clinic" title="Clinic">health clinics</a> to <a href="/wiki/Library" title="Library">libraries</a> to <a href="/wiki/Gym" title="Gym">gymnasiums</a>, to serve the community.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Makeshift_and_temporary_structures_for_Islamic_worship">Makeshift and temporary structures for Islamic worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Makeshift and temporary structures for Islamic worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A temporary place set aside for Islamic worship is called a musalla (<i>Jama'at Khana</i> in South Asia). A musallah is often not part of a permanent endowment (waqf), or it is otherwise not intended to become a permanent legal masjid (as defined in the Sharia). Often musallas are used while a community looks for a piece of land for a permanent masjid, or the establishment of a masjid is not practical at the time. They could be located in rented apartments, industrial units or store fronts.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rules_and_etiquette">Rules and etiquette</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Rules and etiquette">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mosques, in accordance with Islamic practices, institute a number of rules intended to keep Muslims focused on worshiping <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>. While there are several rules, such as those regarding not allowing shoes in the prayer hall, that are universal, there are many other rules that are dealt with and enforced in a variety of ways from mosque to mosque.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prayer_leader">Prayer leader</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Prayer leader">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Prayer_Hall.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1488" data-file-width="1984" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Prayer_Hall.JPG/220px-Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Prayer_Hall.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Prayer_Hall.JPG/330px-Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Prayer_Hall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Prayer_Hall.JPG/440px-Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Prayer_Hall.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taipei_Grand_Mosque_-_Prayer_Hall.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The prayer room inside the Taipei Grand Mosque in Taiwan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Appointment of a prayer leader is considered desirable, but not always obligatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup> The permanent prayer leader (imam) must be a free honest individual and is authoritative in religious matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup> In mosques constructed and maintained by the government, the prayer leader is appointed by the ruler;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup> in private mosques, however, appointment is made by members of the congregation through <a href="/wiki/Majority_rule" title="Majority rule">majority voting</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madh%27hab" title="Madh'hab">school</a> of Islamic jurisprudence, the individual who built the mosque has a stronger claim to the title of imam, but this view is not shared by the other schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Leadership at prayer falls into three categories, depending on the type of prayer: five daily prayers, Friday prayer, or optional prayers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-4"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup> According to the Hanafi and <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> school of Islamic jurisprudence, appointment of a prayer leader for Friday service is mandatory because otherwise the prayer is invalid. The <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" title="Shafi'i">Shafi'i</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a> schools, however, argue that the appointment is not necessary and the prayer is valid as long as it is performed in a congregation. A slave may lead a Friday prayer, but Muslim authorities disagree over whether the job can be done by a minor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-5"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup> An imam appointed to lead Friday prayers may also lead at the five daily prayers; <a href="/wiki/List_of_Islamic_studies_scholars" title="List of Islamic studies scholars">Muslim scholars</a> agree to the leader appointed for five daily services may lead the Friday service as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-6"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>All Muslim authorities hold the consensus opinion that only men may lead prayer for men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-7"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup> Nevertheless, women prayer leaders are allowed to lead prayer in front of all-female congregations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cleanliness">Cleanliness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Cleanliness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purity_in_Islam" title="Ritual purity in Islam">Ritual purity in Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zoetermeer_Meerzicht_Moskee_Qibla_(04).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2627" data-file-width="1971" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Zoetermeer_Meerzicht_Moskee_Qibla_%2804%29.JPG/170px-Zoetermeer_Meerzicht_Moskee_Qibla_%2804%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Zoetermeer_Meerzicht_Moskee_Qibla_%2804%29.JPG/255px-Zoetermeer_Meerzicht_Moskee_Qibla_%2804%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Zoetermeer_Meerzicht_Moskee_Qibla_%2804%29.JPG/340px-Zoetermeer_Meerzicht_Moskee_Qibla_%2804%29.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zoetermeer_Meerzicht_Moskee_Qibla_(04).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Storage for shoes</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All mosques have rules regarding cleanliness, as it is an essential part of the worshippers' experience. Muslims before prayer are required to cleanse themselves in an ablution process known as <i>wudu</i>. However, even to those who enter the prayer hall of a mosque without the intention of praying, there are still rules that apply. Shoes must not be worn inside the carpeted prayer hall. Some mosques will also extend that rule to include other parts of the facility even if those other locations are not devoted to prayer. Congregants and visitors to mosques are supposed to be clean themselves. It is also undesirable to come to the mosque after eating something that smells, such as garlic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dress">Dress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Dress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Islam requires that its adherents wear <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_clothing" title="Islam and clothing">clothes</a> that portray <a href="/wiki/Modesty" title="Modesty">modesty</a>. Men are supposed to come to the mosque wearing loose and clean clothes that do not reveal the shape of the body. Likewise, it is recommended that women at a mosque wear loose clothing that covers to the wrists and ankles, and cover their heads with a <i><a href="/wiki/Hijab" title="Hijab">hijab</a></i> or other covering. Many Muslims, regardless of their ethnic background, wear Middle Eastern clothing associated with Arabic Islam to special occasions and prayers at mosques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teach-islam_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-teach-islam-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inside_Tippu_Mosque.Mysore.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2011" data-file-width="1555" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Inside_Tippu_Mosque.Mysore.jpg/220px-Inside_Tippu_Mosque.Mysore.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Inside_Tippu_Mosque.Mysore.jpg/330px-Inside_Tippu_Mosque.Mysore.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Inside_Tippu_Mosque.Mysore.jpg/440px-Inside_Tippu_Mosque.Mysore.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inside_Tippu_Mosque.Mysore.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tippu Mosque, Mysore</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concentration">Concentration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Concentration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As mosques are places of worship, those within the mosque are required to remain respectful to those in prayer. Loud talking within the mosque, as well as discussion of topics deemed disrespectful, is forbidden in areas where people are praying. In addition, it is disrespectful to walk in front of or otherwise disturb Muslims in prayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The walls within the mosque have few items, except for possibly Islamic calligraphy, so Muslims in prayer are not distracted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Muslims are also discouraged from wearing clothing with distracting <a href="/wiki/Image" title="Image">images</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbols</a> so as not to divert the attention of those standing behind them during prayer. In many mosques, even the carpeted prayer area has no designs, its plainness helping worshippers to focus.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_separation">Gender separation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Gender separation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_segregation_and_Islam" title="Gender segregation and Islam">Gender segregation and Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sultan_Abdul_Majid_mosque_in_Byblos,_Lebanon_(for_women_only).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Sultan_Abdul_Majid_mosque_in_Byblos%2C_Lebanon_%28for_women_only%29.JPG/220px-Sultan_Abdul_Majid_mosque_in_Byblos%2C_Lebanon_%28for_women_only%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Sultan_Abdul_Majid_mosque_in_Byblos%2C_Lebanon_%28for_women_only%29.JPG/330px-Sultan_Abdul_Majid_mosque_in_Byblos%2C_Lebanon_%28for_women_only%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Sultan_Abdul_Majid_mosque_in_Byblos%2C_Lebanon_%28for_women_only%29.JPG/440px-Sultan_Abdul_Majid_mosque_in_Byblos%2C_Lebanon_%28for_women_only%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sultan_Abdul_Majid_mosque_in_Byblos,_Lebanon_(for_women_only).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A women-only mosque in <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is nothing written in the Qurʼan about the issue of space in mosques and gender separation. However, traditional rules have segregated women and men. By traditional rules, women are most often told to occupy the rows behind the men. In part, this was a practical matter as the traditional posture for prayer – kneeling on the floor, head to the ground – made mixed-gender prayer uncomfortably revealing for many women and distracting for some men. Traditionalists try to argue that Muhammad preferred women to pray at home rather than at a mosque, and they cite a <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> in which Muhammad supposedly said: "The best mosques for women are the inner parts of their houses," although women were active participants in the mosque started by Muhammad. Muhammad told Muslims not to forbid women from entering mosques. They are allowed to go in. The second Sunni caliph ʻ<a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> at one time prohibited women from attending mosques especially at night because he feared they may be sexually harassed or assaulted by men, so he required them to pray at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Sometimes a special part of the mosque was railed off for women; for example, the governor of Mecca in 870 had ropes tied between the columns to make a separate place for women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masdjid1_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-Masdjid1-30">[30]</a></sup> Many mosques today will put the women behind a barrier or partition or in another room. Mosques in South and Southeast Asia put men and women in separate rooms, as the divisions were built into them centuries ago. In nearly two-thirds of American mosques, women pray behind partitions or in separate areas, not in the main prayer hall; some mosques do not admit women at all due to the lack of space and the fact that some prayers, such as the Friday Jumuʻah, are mandatory for men but optional for women. Although there are sections exclusively for women and children, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Haram" title="Masjid al-Haram">Grand Mosque</a> in Mecca is desegregated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-Muslims_in_mosques">Non-Muslims in mosques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mosque&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Non-Muslims in mosques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_and_Michelle_Obama_at_the_Istiqlal_Mosque,_Jakarta,_Indonesia,_Nov._10,_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4096" data-file-width="2731" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Barack_Obama_and_Michelle_Obama_at_the_Istiqlal_Mosque%2C_Jakarta%2C_Indonesia%2C_Nov._10%2C_2010.jpg/220px-Barack_Obama_and_Michelle_Obama_at_the_Istiqlal_Mosque%2C_Jakarta%2C_Indonesia%2C_Nov._10%2C_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Barack_Obama_and_Michelle_Obama_at_the_Istiqlal_Mosque%2C_Jakarta%2C_Indonesia%2C_Nov._10%2C_2010.jpg/330px-Barack_Obama_and_Michelle_Obama_at_the_Istiqlal_Mosque%2C_Jakarta%2C_Indonesia%2C_Nov._10%2C_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Barack_Obama_and_Michelle_Obama_at_the_Istiqlal_Mosque%2C_Jakarta%2C_Indonesia%2C_Nov._10%2C_2010.jpg/440px-Barack_Obama_and_Michelle_Obama_at_the_Istiqlal_Mosque%2C_Jakarta%2C_Indonesia%2C_Nov._10%2C_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_and_Michelle_Obama_at_the_Istiqlal_Mosque,_Jakarta,_Indonesia,_Nov._10,_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Obama" title="Michelle Obama">Michelle Obama</a> at the Istiqlal Mosque, <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under most interpretations of <i>sharia</i>, non-Muslims are permitted to enter mosques provided that they respect the place and the people inside it. <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> once allowed a group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> to pray inside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Masjid_al-Nabawi" title="Al-Masjid al-Nabawi">Al-Masjid al-Nabawi</a> for their <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Day" title="Lord's Day">Sunday worship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title=" Dead link since November 2016">dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs additional references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">additional citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A dissenting opinion and minority view is presented by followers of the <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> school of Islamic jurisprudence, who argue that non-Muslims may not be allowed into mosques under any circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mawardi112_74-8"><a href="#cite_note-mawardi112-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran addresses the subject of non-Muslims, and particularly <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheists</a>, in mosques in two verses in its ninth chapter, <a href="/wiki/At-Tawba" title="At-Tawba">Sura At-Tawba</a>. The seventeenth verse of the chapter prohibits those who <i>join gods with Allah</i>—polytheists—from entering mosques:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is not for such as join gods with Allah, to visit or maintain the mosques of Allah while they witness against their own souls to infidelity. The works of such bear no fruit: In Fire shall they dwell.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Quran, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sura" title="Sura">Sura</a></i> 9 (At-Tawba), <i><a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">ayah</a></i> 17<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>The twenty-eighth verse of the same chapter is more specific as it only considers polytheists in the Sacred Mosque, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Haram" title="Masjid al-Haram">Masjid al-Haram</a> in Mecca:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>O ye who believe! Truly the Pagans are unclean; so let them not, after this year of theirs, approach the Sacred Mosque. And if ye fear poverty, soon will Allah enrich you, if He wills, out of His bounty, for Allah is All-knowing, All-wise.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Quran, Sura 9 (At-Tawba), ayah 28<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a>, these verses were followed to the letter at the times of Muhammad, when <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> and Christians, considered <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheists</a>, were still allowed to the Masjid al-Haram. However, the Umayyad caliph <a href="/wiki/Umar_II" title="Umar II">Umar II</a> later forbade non-Muslims from entering mosques, and his ruling remains in practice in present-day Saudi Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masdjid1_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-Masdjid1-30">[30]</a></sup> Today, the decision on whether non-Muslims should be allowed to enter mosques varies. With few exceptions, mosques in the Arabian Peninsula as well as Morocco do not allow entry to non-Muslims. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Hassan_II_Mosque" title="Hassan II Mosque">Hassan II Mosque</a> in Casablanca is one of only two mosques in Morocco currently open to non-Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bush_Islamic_Center_Washington.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bush_Islamic_Center_Washington.jpg/220px-Bush_Islamic_Center_Washington.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bush_Islamic_Center_Washington.jpg/330px-Bush_Islamic_Center_Washington.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Bush_Islamic_Center_Washington.jpg/440px-Bush_Islamic_Center_Washington.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bush_Islamic_Center_Washington.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> inside the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Center_of_Washington" title="Islamic Center of Washington">Islamic Center of Washington</a>, D.C.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, there are also many other places in the West as well as the Islamic world where non-Muslims are welcome to enter mosques. Most mosques in the United States, for example, report receiving non-Muslim visitors every month. Many mosques throughout the United States welcome non-Muslims as a sign of openness to the rest of the community as well as to encourage conversions to Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>In modern-day Saudi Arabia, the Grand Mosque and all of Mecca are open only to Muslims. Likewise, the Al-Masjid al-Nabawi and the city of <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> that surrounds it are also off-limits to those who do not practice Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> For mosques in other areas, it has most commonly been taken that non-Muslims may only enter mosques if granted permission to do so by Muslims and if they have a legitimate reason. All entrants regardless of <a href="/wiki/List_of_religions_and_spiritual_traditions" title="List of religions and spiritual traditions">religious affiliation</a> are expected to respect the rules and <a href="/wiki/Decorum" title="Decorum">decorum</a> for mosques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teach-islam_35-6"><a href="#cite_note-teach-islam-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Badshahi_Mosque_July_1_2005_pic32_by_Ali_Imran_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1514" data-file-width="2270" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Badshahi_Mosque_July_1_2005_pic32_by_Ali_Imran_%281%29.jpg/220px-Badshahi_Mosque_July_1_2005_pic32_by_Ali_Imran_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Badshahi_Mosque_July_1_2005_pic32_by_Ali_Imran_%281%29.jpg/330px-Badshahi_Mosque_July_1_2005_pic32_by_Ali_Imran_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Badshahi_Mosque_July_1_2005_pic32_by_Ali_Imran_%281%29.jpg/440px-Badshahi_Mosque_July_1_2005_pic32_by_Ali_Imran_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Badshahi_Mosque_July_1_2005_pic32_by_Ali_Imran_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Badshahi_Mosque" title="Badshahi Mosque">Badshahi Mosque</a> (Royal Mosque) in <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a>, Pakistan, built by <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a>, is open to non-Muslim tourists.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In modern Turkey, non-Muslim tourists are allowed to enter any mosque, but there are some strict rules. Visiting a mosque is allowed only between prayers; visitors are required to wear long trousers and not to wear shoes, women must cover their heads; visitors are not allowed to interrupt praying Muslims, especially by taking photos of them; no loud talk is allowed; and no references to other religions are allowed (no crosses on necklaces, no cross gestures, etc.) Similar rules apply to mosques in Malaysia, where larger mosques that are also tourist attractions (such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Mosque_of_Malaysia" title="National Mosque of Malaysia">Masjid Negara</a>) provide robes and headscarves for visitors who are deemed inappropriately attired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In certain times and places, non-Muslims were expected to behave a certain way in the vicinity of a mosque: in some Moroccan cities, Jews were required to remove their shoes when passing by a mosque;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> in 18th-century Egypt, Jews and Christians had to dismount before several mosques in veneration of their sanctity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malta_-_Paola_-_Triq_Kordin_-_Mosque_01_ies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Malta_-_Paola_-_Triq_Kordin_-_Mosque_01_ies.jpg/220px-Malta_-_Paola_-_Triq_Kordin_-_Mosque_01_ies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Malta_-_Paola_-_Triq_Kordin_-_Mosque_01_ies.jpg/330px-Malta_-_Paola_-_Triq_Kordin_-_Mosque_01_ies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Malta_-_Paola_-_Triq_Kordin_-_Mosque_01_ies.jpg/440px-Malta_-_Paola_-_Triq_Kordin_-_Mosque_01_ies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malta_-_Paola_-_Triq_Kordin_-_Mosque_01_ies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mariam_Al-Batool_Mosque" title="Mariam Al-Batool Mosque">Mariam Al-Batool Mosque</a> is the only Mosque in <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> and is open to everyone but during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friday_prayers" title="Friday prayers">Friday prayers</a> women are segregated to nearby rooms of the Islamic Center</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The mosque played a major part in the spread of education in the Muslim World, and the association of the mosque with education remained one of its main characteristics throughout history, and, the school became an indispensable appendage to the mosque. From the earliest days of Islam, the mosque was the center of the Muslim community, a place for prayer, meditation, religious instruction, political discussion, and a school. Anywhere Islam took hold, mosques were established; and basic religious and educational instruction began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
